reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs 1D convolution via FFT for long learnable filters. Computes circular convolution by
    zero-padding to avoid wraparound, performing FFT on both input and filter, multiplying in
    frequency domain, and inverse FFT. This is the core computational primitive in Hyena, H3,
    S4, and other SSM-based architectures for subquadratic sequence mixing.

    Args:
        channels (int): Number of independent channels (each has its own filter).
        seq_len (int): Length of input sequences.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels: int, seq_len: int):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.channels = channels
        self.seq_len = seq_len
        self.fft_size = 2 * seq_len
        self.filter = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(channels, seq_len))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        B, C, L = x.shape
        x_padded = torch.nn.functional.pad(x, (0, self.seq_len))
        k_padded = torch.nn.functional.pad(self.filter, (0, self.seq_len))
        x_f = torch.fft.rfft(x_padded, dim=-1)
        k_f = torch.fft.rfft(k_padded, dim=-1)
        out_f = x_f * k_f.unsqueeze(0)
        out = torch.fft.irfft(out_f, n=self.fft_size, dim=-1)
        return out[..., :self.seq_len]

batch_size = 16
channels = 256
seq_len = 8192

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, seq_len]

We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation of a PyTorch model. The model performs 1D convolution via FFT with zero-padding: pad input and filter to length 2*seq_len, apply real FFT (rfft), multiply in frequency domain, then inverse rfft and truncate back to seq_len. We need to produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision (atol=rtol=1e-4). We need to make it faster than PyTorch reference. The key idea: PyTorch internally uses cuFFT for FFT. However, for small filter lengths (e.g., 8192), perhaps we could implement a direct convolution in time domain? The model uses FFT-based convolution. The filter length is seq_len (8192), input length is seq_len (8192). Linear convolution via FFT with zero-padding to 2*seq_len (16384). FFT size 16384. Alternatively, we could implement a custom CUDA kernel that performs the FFT using the Cooley-Tukey algorithm (maybe a radix-2 or mixed-radix) directly on the device? But implementing a high-performance FFT from scratch is extremely difficult. However, note that the length is 16384, which is a power of 2. Also the filter is per-channel and static (parameter), and the input varies per forward call. But perhaps we can do something smarter: since the filter is a parameter of shape (channels, seq_len), we could precompute its FFT (k_f) during initialization or during the forward call. In the reference, k_f is computed every forward call (self.filter padded then rfft). If we precompute k_f as a buffer, we can avoid recomputing the FFT of the filter each forward pass. However, because filter is a learnable parameter, we need to recompute the FFT whenever the filter changes (i.e., during training). But the reference implementation does compute it every forward pass, so our custom kernel must produce the same output for the same parameters. We can precompute and update it, but that adds overhead. However, the evaluation likely just does a forward pass (maybe multiple). In PyTorch, if we register a buffer and compute it in forward, we need to recompute when the parameter changes. Doing direct convolution (O(n^2)) for each channel would be 8192^2 = 67M multiplications per channel, times 256 channels = 17B multiplications, times batch = 16 => 274B multiplications. That's too heavy. FFT-based convolution is O(n log n) = 8192*14 ≈ 115k per channel, times 256*16 ≈ 470M operations. So FFT is much faster. Wait, reference does linear convolution (zero-padded to 2n): output[n] = sum_{i=0}^{n} x[i] * h[n-i] for n = 0..seq_len-1. Since both are length seq_len, the output of length seq_len is the first seq_len elements of the linear convolution. So output[n] = sum_{i=0}^{n} x[i] * h[n-i]. That's a causal convolution (with no dilation). Equivalent to applying a lower-triangular matrix multiplication: y = L * x, where L[n,i] = h[n-i] for i <= n, else 0. So it's a lower-triangular Toeplitz matrix-vector product. Could we implement this as a batched matrix-vector product using a custom kernel? The input and filter are length 8192. Linear convolution result length 16383. We only need first 8192. Is there a way to compute this using FFTs of size 8192 with some pre/post-processing? Let's think. If we compute the circular convolution of length 8192 (i.e., FFT size 8192), we get the linear convolution modulo 8192. The linear convolution for index n is: y_lin[n] = sum_{i=0}^{n} x[i] * h[n-i] for n < 8192. y_circ[n] = sum_{i=0}^{8191} x[i] * h[(n-i) mod 8192]. For n < 8192, the difference is that when n-i < 0, i.e., i > n, the term wraps around: h[n-i+8192] instead of 0. So: y_circ[n] = y_lin[n] + sum_{i=n+1}^{8191} x[i] * h[n-i+8192]. But h[j] for j >= 8192 is zero (since filter length is 8192). Wait, original filter h is length 8192, indices 0..8191. In the linear convolution, we used h[n-i] where n-i ranges from 0..n. In the wrap-around term, n-i+8192 = (n+8192) - i. Since i <= 8191, (n+8192)-i >= n+1 > 8191? Let's see: max i = 8191, n max = 8191. Then n-i+8192 = 8192. That's out of bounds for h (which is length 8192). So the wrap-around term always indexes h at indices >= 8192, which are zero (if we zero-extend h). Wait, but in circular convolution of length 8192, the filter is treated as periodic with period 8192. So h[j mod 8192] = h[j] for j < 8192, and h[j] for j >= 8192 wraps to h[j-8192]. For j = n-i+8192, since n-i >= -(8191), j ranges from 1 to 8191+8192=16383. For j >= 8192, j-8192 ranges from 0 to 8191. So the wrap-around terms involve h[0..8191] again, not zero. Specifically: y_circ[n] = sum_{i=0}^{n} x[i] * h[n-i] + sum_{i=n+1}^{8191} x[i] * h[n-i+8192]. Since h is defined for indices 0..8191, and n-i+8192 >= 8192 - (8191-n) = n+1 > 0, and max is n+8192. For n=8191, i=8191 => j = 8192 => h[0]. So yes, wrap-around uses h[0..8191]. Thus circular convolution is not equal to linear convolution for the first N points; it's contaminated by wrap-around. For N=8192, 2N log(2N) = 16384 * 14 = 229k ops. Three N log N = 3 * 8192 * 13 = 319k ops. So it's worse. What about using FFT size N for circular convolution and then correcting the aliasing? The aliasing in circular convolution of length N is the sum of the "tail" contributions. Specifically, for two length-N sequences a and b, the circular convolution c[n] = sum_{k=0}^{N-1} a[k] * b[(n-k) mod N]. The linear convolution l[n] = sum_{k=0}^{n} a[k] * b[n-k] for n < N. The difference is: c[n] = l[n] + sum_{k=n+1}^{N-1} a[k] * b[n-k+N]. Let d[n] = sum_{k=n+1}^{N-1} a[k] * b[n-k+N] for n = 0..N-2, and d[N-1] = 0. If we can compute d efficiently, then l = c - d. How to compute d? Notice that d[n] = sum_{k=0}^{N-1-n} a[k+n+1] * b[N-1-k]. This is a cross-correlation of the reversed tail of a with the reversed tail of b? Actually, define a_rev[i] = a[N-1-i], b_rev[i] = b[N-1-i]. Then d[n] = sum_{k=0}^{N-1-n} a_rev[N-1-(k+n+1)] * b_rev[k] = sum_{k=0}^{N-1-n} a_rev[N-2-n-k] * b_rev[k]. That's a convolution of a_rev and b_rev evaluated at index N-2-n. So d = reverse(convolution(a_rev, b_rev))[0:N-1]. The convolution of a_rev and b_rev is length 2N-1. We need indices N-2 down to 0. That's the first N-1 points of the reversed convolution. This doesn't simplify. Alternatively, d[n] is the first N-1 terms of the linear convolution of a[N-1:0:-1] and b[N-1::-1] reversed. This is as hard as the original problem. What about using a number-theoretic transform (NTT) mod x^N? No. So the best approach is to stick with FFT size 2N. Let's outline the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <ATen/ATen.h> #include <c10/util/complex.h> __global__ void complex_mul_kernel( c10::complex<float>* __restrict__ out, const c10::complex<float>* __restrict__ x_f, const c10::complex<float>* __restrict__ k_f, int batch, int channels, int nfft) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch * channels * nfft; if (idx >= total) return; int m = idx % nfft; int tmp = idx / nfft; int c = tmp % channels; int b = tmp / channels; // k_f is (channels, nfft), x_f is (batch, channels, nfft) c10::complex<float> a = x_f[b * channels * nfft + c * nfft + m]; c10::complex<float> b_ = k_f[c * nfft + m]; out[idx] = a * b_; } torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor filter_fft, int64_t seq_len) { int64_t fft_size = 2 * seq_len; // x: (B, C, L) auto x_f = at::fft_rfft(x, c10::nullopt, -1, c10::nullopt); // wait, we need to pass n=fft_size. For a custom Cooley-Tukey radix-2 FFT in shared memory: - Size 16384 real -> 8192 complex - We can process as 8192-point complex FFT using the real-input packing trick - 8192 = 2^13 - Each stage has 4096 butterflies - A butterfly does a complex multiply-add If each thread block has 256 threads, and we partition the butterflies among threads, each thread does 16 butterflies per stage. There are 13 stages. - Total work per thread: 13 * 16 = 208 complex butterflies - Each complex butterfly is ~4 FMAs (for a cooley-tukey with twiddle), so ~832 FMAs per thread - Not a lot of compute. The kernel would be memory-bound within shared memory. Shared memory bandwidth is ~19 TB/s per SM on H100. With 132 SMs, that's plenty. But with only 1 block per SM (due to shared memory limit), we're launching 132 blocks at a time. Each block processes one length-16384 FFT. Time for one block: staged FFT in shared memory. Each of the 13 stages requires reading and writing the entire array in shared memory. That's 13 * 8192 complex * 8 bytes = ~850KB of shared memory traffic per FFT. At 19TB/s per SM, that's about 45 microseconds per FFT. With 132 concurrent FFTs: 132 * 45us = 5940us for 132 FFTs. For 4096 FFTs: 4096 / 132 * 45us ≈ 1395us = 1.4ms. This is competitive but maybe not faster than cuFFT. And I haven't accounted for the inverse FFT or the filter FFT. cuFFT for 4096 x 16384 R2C might be in the sub-millisecond range. Multiply: read x_f, k_f (256+16MB), write out_f (256MB) 6. irfft: read out_f (256MB), write out (256MB) Total device memory traffic for "naive" separate tensors: well over 1.5TB/s if done quickly. Each batch has different x. Let me look at this from a very practical standpoint. I'll implement a version that uses a custom FFT and see how it performs. For the FFT, I'll use a radix-2 Cooley-Tukey, but optimize it for shared memory. For length 8192 complex FFT (which we need for the R2C transform): - 8192 = 2^13 - We can process it with 256 threads, each handling 32 complex numbers in a register/cache hybrid approach. Actually, a more modern approach is to use the Stockham autosort algorithm, which avoids the bit-reversal permutation. Each stage is a uniform access pattern. For a shared-memory implementation: 1. Load data from global memory into shared memory (or registers) 2. For each stage, read from one SMEM buffer, compute butterflies, write to another SMEM buffer 3. Ping-pong between two buffers With 8192 complex numbers = 8192 * 8 = 65536 bytes per buffer. Two buffers = 131072 bytes. This fits in shared memory! So each thread block can hold two full arrays of 8192 complex in shared memory. With 256 threads per block, each thread handles 32 complex points. For 13 stages, we do ping-pong: - Stage 0: read buf0, compute butterflies with stride 1, distance 2, write buf1 - Stage 1: read buf1, stride 2, distance 4, write buf0 - etc. At the end, we read from the appropriate buffer and write to global memory. Wait, but we also need twiddle factors. For each stage s (0 to 12), the twiddle W_N^{k} for k varying. We can compute twiddles on the fly using `__sincosf` or load precomputed tables. Precomputing in shared memory: 8192 complex twiddles = 64KB. But we only need N/2 unique twiddles per stage. Actually, for each stage, the number of unique twiddles is the group size. Total unique twiddles for all stages: 1 + 2 + 4 + ... + 4096 = 8191. That's 8191 complex = 64KB. We could store all twiddles in shared memory or in constant memory, or compute on the fly. For 8192 of size 8192, precomputing in shared memory is possible but tight. Computing on the fly with `sincosf` is slower. Actually, for a fixed size N=8192, we can precompute twiddles at module load and pass them as a constant buffer. In CUDA, we can pass the twiddle table as a kernel argument (device memory), and each thread loads what it needs. Since the access pattern is uniform (all threads in a warp load the same twiddle or sequential ones), L1 cache should handle it well. To avoid shared memory bank conflicts, we should organize the arrays with complexity in mind. For a butterfly at distance D in the first stages, threads are strided by 1, so consecutive threads access consecutive memory locations, which is bank-conflict-free. For later stages with large strides, threads might access memory with a stride, causing bank conflicts. To handle this, we can transpose the data in shared memory between stages or use a different indexing. In Stockham, we write output such that for the next stage, consecutive elements are adjacent. Specifically, Stockham algorithm naturally produces the output in the right order if we access correctly. Actually, let me be more precise. In the Stockham algorithm: - Input: interleaved (natural order) - At each stage, we process pairs/groups and write output in a different order - Specifically, for decimation-in-frequency or decimation-in-time variants For DIT Stockham: - Stage s processes groups of 2^{s+1} points - Within each group, butterflies connect points distance 2^s apart - Output is written with the lower half of each group going to even indices and upper half to odd indices (or vice versa) - This naturally avoids bit-reversal at the end The memory access pattern: within a butterfly group, each thread can process one or more butterflies. The read pattern has stride 2^s, the write pattern has stride 2^{s-1} for the two halves. For stage s (0-indexed): - Group size G = 2^{s+1} - Number of groups = N / G - Butterflies per group = G / 2 = 2^s - Each butterfly connects i and i + G/2 within the group In Stockham, we might have each thread process butterflies contiguous in the group, or contiguous across groups. To avoid bank conflicts, we want threads to access consecutive memory banks. If thread t processes butterfly t, then for stage s, the two reads are at offset t and t + 2^s. If t increments by 1 across threads, then thread 0 reads at 0 and 2^s, thread 1 reads at 1 and 1+2^s, etc. These are consecutive+gapped, which causes 2-way bank conflicts when 2^s is a multiple of 32 (for s >= 5). A common solution is to have each thread process multiple butterflies, or to transpose between stages. Alternatively, we can use the "Four-step" or "Six-step" algorithm that breaks large FFTs into smaller ones + transposes. Given the complexity, and that cuFFT has already solved all these problems, I'm not confident a custom FFT can easily beat it. Let me try yet another approach. The total parallelism across 4096 transforms is huge. What if I examine whether there's a better FFT size? 16384 is 2^14. Can we use Bluestein's algorithm to do a length-8192 FFT instead? No, we need 16384 for zero padding. Actually wait. x is length 8192, we zero pad to 16384. But in frequency domain, x_f[0] and x_f[8192] are real (Nyquist terms). The complex output is 8193 values. Could we use the older approach of computing a length-8192 complex FFT on interleaved data to get two real FFTs? Yes! We could pack two real sequences into one complex sequence. Since we have B*C = 4096 real sequences to transform, we can pair them up and do 2048 complex FFTs of length 8192. Then we use the post-processing formulas to extract two real FFTs from each complex FFT. This halves the number of forward FFTs from 4096 to 2048! Similarly for the inverse, we can pair up the 4096 complex frequency domain sequences into 2048 complex IFFT inputs. But the inverse is a bit different: we need to combine before the IFFT. Let's see: - Forward: pair x[b1, c1, :] and x[b2, c2, :] as real and imaginary parts of a complex sequence z. Compute FFT(z) of length 16384 (complex). Extract X1[k] and X2[k]. - But wait, we need R2C of length 16384. The packing trick for two real sequences works with C2C of length N/2 or using the real-complex packing algorithm. Actually, the standard trick for two real sequences uses a single complex FFT of the SAME length: 1. z[n] = x1[n] + j * x2[n] 2. Z[k] = FFT(z) 3. X1[k] = 0.5 * (Z[k] + conj(Z[N-k])) 4. X2[k] = -0.5j * (Z[k] - conj(Z[N-k])) So we do 2048 complex FFTs of length 16384 instead of 4096 real FFTs of length 16384. That's a 2x reduction in forward FFTs! Similarly for the inverse, we can combine two inverse FFTs into one. For the inverse: if we have Y1[k] and Y2[k], we form Z[k] = Y1[k] + j*Y2[k], do IFFT(Z), and extract the real parts. Wait, but there's a subtlety. The filter k is also a real sequence. Could we pair each filter with a batch? The filter has C=256 channels. The input has B*C=4096 channels. We could pair 256 filters with 256 input channels and do 256 complex FFTs, then do the remaining 3840 input channels as 1920 more complex FFTs. But actually, we only need to do the filter FFT once! And we have 4096 input channels to transform. Wait, no. The input has B*C = 4096 independent sequences. The filter has C=256 sequences, but each of the B=16 batches uses the SAME C filters. So there are B*C = 4096 convolutions to compute, but only 256 unique filters. Forward FFTs needed: - 4096 for input x - 256 for filter k - Total: 4352 If we pair x sequences: 4096 / 2 = 2048 complex FFTs. For filter: 256 / 2 = 128 complex FFTs. Total: 2176 complex FFTs of length 16384. For inverse: 4096 sequences. We can pair them: 2048 complex IFFTs of length 16384. Total FFTs: 2176 + 2048 = 4224 complex FFTs of length 16384. vs original: 4352 + 4096 = 8448 FFTs (mix of R2C and C2R). Wait, but a complex FFT of length 16384 is more expensive than a real FFT of length 16384, but less than 2x. R2C exploits symmetry to be roughly 2x faster than C2C of same length. So 2048 complex FFTs ≈ 4096 real FFTs in cost. For the forward direction, we save exactly nothing by pairing! The standard result is: two real N-point FFTs via one complex N-point FFT has the same asymptotic cost but with slightly better constant factors due to fewer kernel launches. For the inverse, same thing. So the pairing trick gives marginal improvement at best, not 2x. Hmm, what if we do the multiplication in the complex domain before the IFFT? For each pair of output sequences, we'd need to form: z_f[k] = (x1_f[k] * k_f[c1,k]) + j * (x2_f[k] * k_f[c2,k]) This requires the frequency-domain multiplies before we can combine. So we'd still need to do all the x_f and k_f first, then multiply, then pair for inverse. The saves are: - Forward: 4096 R2C -> 2048 C2C (saves ~2048 kernel launches + some compute) - Backward: 4096 C2R -> 2048 C2C (saves ~2048 launches) Potentially a win from fewer kernel launches and slightly better efficiency. For output length m and filter size r, Winograd reduces the problem to FFT of size m+r-1. But it does multiple small FFTs instead of one large FFT. For our case, m=8192, r=8192, so FFT size is 16384. Looking back at the code: ```python self.filter = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(channels, seq_len)) ``` So yes, filter is [channels, seq_len] = [256, 8192]. And x is [batch_size, channels, seq_len] = [16, 256, 8192]. Both are length 8192, and we want their linear convolution padded/truncated to 8192 outputs. Since we're only taking the first 8192 outputs (indices 0 to 8191), and the linear convolution gives indices 0 to 16382, we're truncating. For index i in [0, 8191]: out[i] = sum_{j=0}^{i} x[j] * k[i-j]. Notice that for each i, the filter is only used up to index i. So the filter is effectively being applied in a "causal" or "triangular" manner. Could we compute this using cumulative sums? out[i] = sum_{j=0}^{i} x[j] * k[i-j] Define for each lag l: cross-correlation at lag l = sum x[j] * k[j+l]? Wait, our sum has k[i-j], which is cross-correlation, not convolution (if we define convolution as sum x[j] * k[i-j], this IS convolution). Actually there's a nice matrix factorization. The operation can be written as: out = L * x where L is a lower-triangular Toeplitz matrix with first column k[0], k[1], ..., k[N-1]. Specifically, L[i,j] = k[i-j] for i >= j, else 0. A lower-triangular Toeplitz matrix L can be embedded in a circulant matrix C. The circulant matrix C has first column [k[0], k[1], ..., k[N-1], 0, ..., 0]. C = [L, 0; *, *] And C is diagonalized by the DFT matrix: C = F^{-1} * diag(F * c) * F. So the algorithm in the reference is exactly doing this: Cx = F^{-1}(F(c) .* F(x_padded)). There's no obvious shortcut for this specific structure. OK, I think I've exhausted algorithmic approaches. Let me focus on implementation optimization. Using overlap-add: x is already broken into blocks. Each x-block is convolved with the FULL k, and results are shifted and added. Wait, overlap-add works when the filter is short. Here the filter is long (8192). What about overlap-save? We compute circular convolutions of size L with the filter of length N. If L > N, we can do FFTs of size L and discard the first N-1 outputs. But N=8192, so we'd need L > 8192, say L=16384. Then we process L-N+1 = 8193 new outputs per block. But the input for each block would be L = 16384 samples, overlapping by N-1 = 8191 samples. So each block only gives 8193 new outputs but needs 16384 FFT. The first block gives 8193 outputs (out[0..8192]). And we'd need roughly 1 block for every 8193 outputs, so for 8192 outputs we'd need 1 block. Back to the original FFT size 16384. So no savings there. For the second block (if we wanted outputs beyond 8192), we'd have overlap. But since we only want 8192 outputs, one block suffices. Hmm, what about doing overlap-add where BOTH are broken into small blocks? For two length-N sequences, the Cauchy product can be computed by breaking both into blocks and doing "fast" multiplication on the blocks. This is essentially Karatsuba or Toom-Cook for polynomials. For N = n*m, we can view x and k as degree-(n-1) polynomials in t^m: x(t) = X0(t) + X1(t)*t^m + ... + X_{n-1}(t)*t^{(n-1)m} k(t) = K0(t) + K1(t)*t^m + ... + K_{n-1}(t)*t^{(n-1)m} The product is: out(t) = sum_{i=0}^{n-1} sum_{j=0}^{n-1} Xi(t)*Kj(t)*t^{(i+j)m} For n blocks, there are n^2 block-products. Each block-product is convolution of length-m sequences. Using FFT for each: n^2 FFTs of size 2m. Total cost: n^2 * O(2m log(2m)) = 2n^2 m log(2m) = 2n * N * log(2m). Compare to one FFT of size 2N: cost = O(2N log(2N)). Ratio: [2n * N * log(2m)] / [2N * log(2N)] = n * log(2m) / log(2N) = n * log(2N/n) / log(2N). For n=8, m=1024, N=8192: ratio = 8 * log(2048) / log(16384) = 8 * 11 / 14 ≈ 6.3x SLOWER! So breaking into blocks is worse. The single large FFT is optimal. For n in [0, M-1]: y[n] = sum_{m=0}^{M-1} x[m] * k[n-m] (with k[j] = 0 for j < 0 or j >= M) So y[n] = sum_{m=max(0, n-M+1)}^{min(n, M-1)} x[m] * k[n-m]. But actually, I want to try one more thing. What if instead of one FFT of size 16384, we do the polynomial multiplication using the Montgomery trick or Karatsuba? For polynomials of degree M-1 = 8191: Karatsuba splits each into high and low halves: A = A0 + A1*X^4096, B = B0 + B1*X^4096. A*B = A0*B0 + (A0*B1 + A1*B0)*X^4096 + A1*B1*X^8192. We want the result modulo X^8192, so we discard terms with degree >= 8192. Thus: Result = A0*B0 + (A0*B1 + A1*B0)*X^4096 mod X^8192. Wait, A1*B0 and A0*B1 have degrees up to 4095+4095 = 8190. The term A1*B1*X^8192 would have degree >= 8192, so we drop it. A0*B0 has degree up to 8190. So Result = A0*B0 + (A0*B1 + A1*B0)*X^4096. Hmm, but this is still convolution. A0*B0 is convolution of length 4096, which needs FFT of size 8192. (A0*B1 + A1*B0) is also convolution of length 4096, needing FFT size 8192. And for the middle part, we compute (A0+A1)*(B0+B1) using Karatsuba: A0*B1 + A1*B0 = (A0+A1)*(B0+B1) - A0*B0 - A1*B1. So we need: 1. A0*B0: one FFT of size 8192 2. A1*B1: one FFT of size 8192 (but we actually don't need the result, we just need it for the formula, and A1*B1 has terms of degree 8192-16382, which we don't use for the output) Wait, if we use Karatsuba, we need A1*B1 for the subtraction, but its product has degree up to 8190+8190 = 16382. We can compute it with an FFT of size 16384. Actually, let me recalculate: A0, A1, B0, B1 each have 4096 coefficients. A0*B0: length 8191, needs FFT size 16384 for linear convolution, or 8192 for cyclic (but we need linear). Wait, A0 and B0 are coefficients 0..4095 and 4096..8191 of the original. Their product is needed exactly. For linear convolution of two 4096-length sequences, we need FFT size 8192. Similarly for A0*B1 and A1*B0. Cost: 0.92. So Karatsuba gives virtually IDENTICAL cost for this specific size! The overhead of implementation would make it slower, not faster. Therefore, Karatsuba doesn't help. Hmm, let me think if there's a completely different interpretation of the operation. The reference says: "Performs 1D convolution via FFT for long learnable filters." Could "convolution" mean cross-correlation? The code does `x_f * k_f`, which in frequency domain corresponds to circular convolution, not cross-correlation. For cross-correlation, we'd need `conj(k_f)`. But the reference explicitly says "convolution" and shows `x_f * k_f`. This is correct for convolution when k is the impulse response (no reversal needed because in the time domain, convolution is sum x[n] * k[m-n], which in circular convolution is equivalent to correlation with flipped k. Since k is learned, it can learn the flipped version directly). Wait, looking at the actual code: it doesn't flip k. `torch.nn.functional.pad(self.filter, (0, self.seq_len))` and then rfft. For linear convolution via FFT, we should pad and then multiply. The relationship is: circ_conv(x, k) = IFFT( FFT(x) * FFT(k) ). For the standard convolution where output[i] = sum_j x[j] * k[i-j], the FFT method uses the unpadded k (or padded k without flipping). Actually, the discrete convolution theorem states: x * k (circular) = IDFT( DFT(x) ⊙ DFT(k) ), where ⊙ is elementwise product. For linear convolution, we zero-pad both to length >= len(x)+len(k)-1, and the first len(x)+len(k)-1 samples of the circular convolution give the linear convolution. The reference does exactly this: zero-pad to 2*seq_len, FFT both, multiply, IFFT, truncate to seq_len. Wait, it truncates to seq_len, not seq_len*2-1. For a custom kernel, maybe we should write a direct time-domain convolution kernel. How expensive is a direct convolution for these sizes? Direct convolution: out[i] = sum_{j=0}^{i} x[j] * k[i-j] for i=0..8191. Scalar operations: for each i, i+1 multiplications and i additions. Total: sum_{i=0}^{8191} (i+1) = 8192*8193/2 = 33,550,336 ops per (batch, channel). For 4096 (b,c): 4096 * 33.5M = 137.4B operations. At 67 TFLOPS FP32: ~2 seconds. That's way too slow. But we can parallelize heavily! On a GPU, each thread computes one output point. We have 4096 * 8192 = 33.5M output points. Each output point requires a dot product of two vectors. Wait, 33.5M output points * average 4096 operations per point = 137B operations. At 67 TFLOPS = 2 seconds. Much too slow. What about using shared memory tiles? Like a tiled matrix multiplication? The convolution can be seen as a matrix-vector product with a lower-triangular Toeplitz matrix. We could compute blocks of outputs using shared memory. But it's still O(N^2) total operations. For N=8192, FFT at O(N log N) is ~16384*14 ≈ 229k operations. So FFT is ~146x fewer operations than direct. Direct is hopeless. Unless... we use Tensor Cores with some clever blocking. A lower-triangular Toeplitz can be computed using matrix multiply if we form the appropriate matrices. But the matrices would be N x N, leading to N^3 total work for batched computation if we use standard MM. For 4096 batch items, doing 4096 separate MMs of size 8192x8192 is absurd. Alternatively, we can view it as multiplying a Toeplitz matrix by multiple vectors (the batch dimension). The Toeplitz matrix is the same for all batches in a channel. But we have 256 different Toeplitz matrices (one per channel). For each channel c, we have 16 batches, and the matrix is T_c (8192x8192 lower triangular Toeplitz). We want Y = T_c * X where X is [16, 8192]. This is 16 MMs with the same matrix. We could do cublas batched gemm, but again, the matrix is 8192x8192, and 16 vectors. That's 16 * 8192 * 8192 * 2 = 2.1B flops per channel. For 256 channels: 549B flops. At 67 TFLOPS = 8 seconds. Very slow. So no, direct methods are out. I want to try one more idea. I'll write a C++ CUDA extension with the following features: 1. **Caching k_f**: Since the problem involves a parameter filter, and in inference mode it doesn't change, we can cache its FFT. But also, during training, we can still compute it once per forward. 2. **In-place operations**: Use cufft with in-place buffers where possible. For complex multiply, we can do it in-place on x_f. 3. **Custom elementwise multiply kernel**: This is trivial and avoids PyTorch overhead. 4. **Memory pool/reuse**: Pre-allocate persistent buffers. 5. **Batched FFT via cuFFT**: Call `cufftExecR2C` and `cufftExecC2R` with proper batching. For the FFT, I can call cuFFT from the C++ side. That's allowed. Wait, actually, `load_inline` compiles the C++ code into a shared library. If I include `<cufft.h>` and link with `-lcufft`, I can use cuFFT directly. Let me draft the C++ code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cufft.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor filter, int fft_size, int seq_len) { // x: [B, C, L] // filter: [C, L] int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); // Create plan for x: B*C real FFTs of size fft_size cufftHandle plan_fwd; cufftPlanMany(&plan_fwd, 1, &fft_size, nullptr, 1, fft_size, // inembed, istride, idist -- wait nullptr, 1, fft_size/2+1, // onembed, ostride, odist CUFFT_R2C, B*C); // Output x_f: [B*C, fft_size/2+1] complex64 auto x_f = torch::empty({B*C, fft_size/2+1}, x.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); // Need padded x. We can view x as [B*C, L] and create a padded tensor auto x_padded = torch::zeros({B*C, fft_size}, x.options()); x_padded.narrow(1, 0, L).copy_(x.view({B*C, L})); // Execute FFT cufftExecR2C(plan_fwd, x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), reinterpret_cast<cufftComplex*>(x_f.data_ptr())); // Similar for filter // ... } ``` Wait, `cufftPlanMany` has specific arguments: ```cpp cufftResult cufftPlanMany(cufftHandle *plan, int rank, int *n, int *inembed, int istride, int idist, int *onembed, int ostride, int odist, cufftType type, int batch); ``` For input of shape [B*C, fft_size] where each FFT is contiguous: - rank=1, n=&fft_size - inembed can be NULL (same as n) - istride=1 (consecutive elements) - idist=fft_size (distance between batches) - onembed=NULL - ostride=1 - odist=fft_size/2+1 - type=CUFFT_R2C - batch=B*C Similarly for filter: batch=C. For inverse: input is x_f with shape [B*C, fft_size/2+1], output is out with shape [B*C, fft_size]. - CUFFT_C2R - istride=1, idist=fft_size/2+1 - ostride=1, odist=fft_size - batch=B*C This seems right. Now, for the elementwise multiply: x_f is [B*C, fft_size/2+1], k_f is [C, fft_size/2+1]. For batch b and channel c: x_f_idx = b*C + c, k_f_idx = c. We can write a simple CUDA kernel for this. After multiplication, we do the inverse FFT. For memory reuse: we can do the multiply in-place on x_f, then call C2R with x_f as input. So the flow is: 1. x_padded = pad(x) -- or create zeros and copy 2. x_f = R2C(x_padded) 3. k_f = R2C(k_padded) -- cached or computed 4. Elementwise multiply x_f *= k_f (broadcasted) 5. out_padded = C2R(x_f) 6. Return out_padded[..., :seq_len] To optimize step 1, instead of allocating x_padded and copying x, we could use `torch.empty` and then a custom kernel that copies and zero-pads. Or we could just allocate zeros and do narrow+copy. The narrow+copy is bandwidth-bound and should be fast. Actually, a custom kernel for "copy and pad" could save one memory pass. But let's see if it's necessary. For step 3, if we compute k_f each time: k_padded = zeros([C, fft_size]); k_padded[:, :seq_len] = filter; then R2C. Wait, can we avoid allocating k_padded if we cache k_f? In __init__, we could allocate a persistent buffer for k_f. Then in forward, if filter changed, we update k_f by: 1. Creating k_padded (or using a pre-allocated buffer) 2. Copying filter 3. Doing R2C 4. Storing in k_f buffer. The persistent k_f buffer avoids allocating the output of R2C each time. Similarly, x_f can be a persistent buffer, and out_padded can be a persistent buffer. If we pre-allocate all these buffers in __init__, the forward pass doesn't allocate any new GPU memory for the main tensors. It only allocates temporaries for the FFT workspace, which cuFFT handles internally. Actually, cuFFT might allocate workspace on each plan creation, but we can create the plan once and reuse it. With `cufftSetWorkArea`, we can manage workspace memory too. Persistent plans are a big win! PyTorch might recreate plans each time `torch.fft.rfft` is called. I'm not sure how PyTorch manages FFT plans. In older versions, it used a plan cache. In newer versions, it might still cache plans. But having our own persistent plan avoids any lookup overhead. Also, having our own persistent workspace is a win. Let me draft the ModelNew class: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, seq_len): super().__init__() self.channels = channels self.seq_len = seq_len self.fft_size = 2 * seq_len self.filter = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(channels, seq_len)) # Persistent buffers self.register_buffer('_k_f', torch.empty(channels, seq_len + 1, dtype=torch.complex64)) self.register_buffer('_x_f', torch.empty(1, channels, seq_len + 1, dtype=torch.complex64)) self.register_buffer('_out', torch.empty(1, channels, self.fft_size, dtype=torch.float32)) self._plan_fwd = None self._plan_bwd = None self._plan_k = None def forward(self, x): B, C, L = x.shape ext = self._get_extension() # Lazy init plans and buffers if self._x_f.shape[0] != B: self._x_f = torch.empty(B, C, L + 1, dtype=torch.complex64, device=x.device) self._out = torch.empty(B, C, self.fft_size, dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device) # Create cuFFT plans... result = ext.forward(x, self.filter, self._k_f, self._x_f, self._out, self.fft_size, self.seq_len, self._plan_fwd, ...) return result ``` Hmm, passing cuFFT handles to CUDA is possible but tricky with load_inline. The handles are `cufftHandle` (integers). We could store them as Python integers or in a capsule. Alternatively, we create the plan inside the C++ function each time. But plan creation has overhead. For repeated calls, we want persistent plans. Actually, `cufftHandle` is just an `int`. We can store it in Python and pass it to the C++ function. But the plan needs to be destroyed properly. We can manage this in `__del__` or with a context. Alternatively, we can store the handle in a `torch.Tensor` or Python int. Let's use a Python int stored as `self._plan_handle_x`. Wait, `cufftHandle` is actually `unsigned int` in most implementations. We can pass it as a `int64_t` to Python. Let me think about this more carefully. Plan creation overhead: for size 16384 and batch 4096/256, plan creation might take 1-5ms. If forward is called 1000 times, that's a lot of overhead. So persistent plans are important. BUT, PyTorch has a plan cache! It should reuse plans automatically. So persistent plans might not be a huge win over PyTorch. Still, managing everything manually avoids Python overhead. OK, I'll write a C++ extension and see. But first, let me think about whether there's any other mathematical trick. Actually, I just realized there's one mathematical trick that could help significantly. Since the input x is [B, C, L] and the filter is [C, L], we could combine B and C dimensions into [B*C, L] and the filter into [1, C, L] broadcast to [B, C, L]. But the FFT already handles the dimensions. Hmm, one more idea. In the frequency domain, x_f has shape [B, C, L+1] complex. k_f has shape [C, L+1] complex. x_f * k_f is elementwise over C and freq, broadcast over B. This is a batched elementwise product, which is memory-bandwidth bound. We can fuse it with the IFFT by writing a custom inverse FFT kernel that loads the product into registers and computes the IFFT. So the flow is: 1. R2C of x: [B, C, 16384] -> [B, C, 8193] (via cuFFT or custom) 2. R2C of k: [C, 16384] -> [C, 8193] (cached) 3. For each (b,c): load x_f[b,c,:] and k_f[c,:], multiply, then run IFFT in a custom kernel If we write a custom IFFT kernel that combines the multiply and IFFT, we save one global memory round-trip for writing and reading x_f. The custom IFFT could be a register-shared-memory FFT of size 16384. But as discussed, a high-quality IFFT kernel is hard. However, for the IFFT, we could still use cuFFT! We just need a pre-multiplied input. So: 1. x_f = cufft R2C(x) 2. k_f = cached 3. Custom kernel: x_f[b,c,f] *= k_f[c,f] in-place 4. out = cufft C2R(x_f) This is simple and saves writing a full custom FFT. The speedup would come from: - Persistent k_f - Persistent plans - Minimal Python overhead - In-place multiply avoids extra allocation Can this beat PyTorch? PyTorch likely does: rfft(x) -> x_f, rfft(k) -> k_f, out_f = x_f * k_f, irfft(out_f). PyTorch might allocate x_f, k_f, and out_f separately. If we do in-place on x_f (reusing it as out_f), we save one tensor allocation of size [B, C, 8193] complex = 256MB. That's significant. PyTorch's elementwise multiply might actually use `out=` parameter to avoid allocating out_f. Let's see: `out_f = x_f * k_f.unsqueeze(0)` creates a new tensor. If PyTorch uses broadcasting, it might still need a new output tensor unless we do `torch.mul(x_f, k_f.unsqueeze(0), out=...)` explicitly. The reference uses `out_f = x_f * k_f.unsqueeze(0)`, which definitely allocates a new tensor. So by doing an in-place multiply, we save 256MB of allocation. This allocation takes time: at 3.35 TB/s, allocating zeroed memory takes ~76 microseconds for 256MB. Plus the actual multiply bandwidth. Also, PyTorch's rfft might create a padded copy internally. If we can avoid that too, we save more. Actually, for `torch.fft.rfft(x, n=16384)` with x of shape [B, C, 8192], PyTorch has to allocate an output of [B, C, 8193] and a padded input of [B, C, 16384] (or handle zero-padding internally). In our custom implementation, we can allocate x_padded and zero it once, copy x into it, then run R2C into x_f. This uses well-known memory layout and could be faster. So let me design the custom C++ kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cufft.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // In-place complex multiply with broadcasting __global__ void freq_multiply_kernel( cufftComplex* x_f, // [B, C, L+1] const cufftComplex* k_f, // [C, L+1] int B, int C, int F) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C * F; if (idx >= total) return; int f = idx % F; int c = (idx / F) % C; int b = idx / (F * C); cufftComplex xv = x_f[idx]; cufftComplex kv = k_f[c * F + f]; cufftComplex out; out.x = xv.x * kv.x - xv.y * kv.y; out.y = xv.x * kv.y + xv.y * kv.x; x_f[idx] = out; } torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor k_f, int fft_size, int seq_len) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int F = fft_size / 2 + 1; auto x_padded = torch::zeros({B*C, fft_size}, x.options()); auto x_view = x.view({B*C, L}); x_padded.slice(1, 0, L).copy_(x_view); // FFT x auto x_f = torch::empty({B*C, F}, x.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan; int n = fft_size; cufftPlanMany(&plan, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, fft_size, nullptr, 1, F, CUFFT_R2C, B*C); cufftExecR2C(plan, x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr()); // Multiply freq_multiply_kernel<<<(B*C*F + 255) / 256, 256>>>((cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr(), (cufftComplex*)k_f.data_ptr(), B, C, F); // IFFT auto out_padded = torch::empty({B*C, fft_size}, x.options()); cufftHandle plan_inv; cufftPlanMany(&plan_inv, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, F, nullptr, 1, fft_size, CUFFT_C2R, B*C); cufftExecC2R(plan_inv, (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr(), out_padded.data_ptr<float>()); // Scale by 1/fft_size (cuFFT doesn't normalize) out_padded.div_(fft_size); cufftDestroy(plan); cufftDestroy(plan_inv); return out_padded.view({B, C, fft_size}).narrow(2, 0, seq_len); } torch::Tensor compute_filter_fft(torch::Tensor filter, int fft_size) { int C = filter.size(0); int L = filter.size(1); int F = fft_size / 2 + 1; auto k_padded = torch::zeros({C, fft_size}, filter.options()); k_padded.slice(1, 0, L).copy_(filter); auto k_f = torch::empty({C, F}, filter.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan; int n = fft_size; cufftPlanMany(&plan, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, fft_size, nullptr, 1, F, CUFFT_R2C, C); cufftExecR2C(plan, k_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)k_f.data_ptr()); cufftDestroy(plan); return k_f; } ``` This is a basic version. The key issues: 1. Plan creation on every call is expensive. 2. We need to manage the stream. 3. We need to synchronize appropriately. For production quality: - We should create plans once and reuse them. - We should use the current CUDA stream. - `cufftExecR2C` is asynchronous, but we need to make sure the multiply kernel waits for it. - cuFFT launches are asynchronous in the same stream, so if we queue them in the same stream, they're ordered correctly. But the multiply kernel must also be in the same stream. Wait, cuFFT calls are on the "current" stream or we can set the stream with `cufftSetStream`. PyTorch uses streams, so we should get the current stream and set it on the cuFFT plan. Also, `torch::empty` doesn't zero memory. `torch::zeros` does. For x_padded, we need zeros in the second half. But we allocate all zeros, then copy the first half. This is two passes over memory: one to zero, one to copy. We could optimize by allocating with `torch::empty` and then using a custom kernel that copies x and zeros the padding in one pass. For filter, we can precompute k_f and cache it. Plans: to avoid recreating plans, we can create them in Python and pass the handles, or create them on first call and cache them in the C++ module. But with `load_inline`, each Python process gets one module instance. We can store state at the module level or pass handles from Python. Actually, we can create a "handle" object in C++ that owns the plan and destroy it properly. But for simplicity, let me try creating the plan each time first and see the performance. For batch size 4096 and size 16384, plan creation might not be a huge overhead compared to the actual transforms. But for repeated calls, it could add up. Let me look up cuFFT plan creation time. For 1D transforms of size 16384 with batch 4096, plan creation might take 1-2ms. If the actual FFT takes 0.5ms, then plan creation dominates! So persistent plans are essential. One approach: delay plan creation to first forward call and cache the plan handle in Python. We can do this safely since the device won't change mid-run. ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, seq_len): super().__init__() self.channels = channels self.seq_len = seq_len self.fft_size = 2 * seq_len self.filter = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(channels, seq_len)) self._k_f = None self._plan_x = None self._plan_inv = None self._k_version = -1 def forward(self, x): import custom_fft_conv as _ext B, C, L = x.shape F = self.fft_size // 2 + 1 if self._k_f is None or self._k_version != self.filter._version: self._k_f = _ext.compute_filter_fft(self.filter.contiguous(), self.fft_size) self._k_version = self.filter._version if self._plan_x is None or self._plan_x_batch != B * C: if self._plan_x is not None: _ext.destroy_plan(self._plan_x) self._plan_x = _ext.create_r2c_plan(B*C, self.fft_size) self._plan_x_batch = B * C if self._plan_inv is None or self._plan_inv_batch != B * C: if self._plan_inv is not None: _ext.destroy_plan(self._plan_inv) self._plan_inv = _ext.create_c2r_plan(B*C, self.fft_size) self._plan_inv_batch = B * C return _ext.forward_cuda(x.contiguous(), self._k_f, self._plan_x, self._plan_inv, self.fft_size, self.seq_len) ``` This is getting complex but feasible. But first, I want to try one more thing: can I actually write a self-contained FFT that's competitive for this size? For length 16384 real -> 8193 complex, I can use a register-based FFT algorithm. With 1024 threads per block: - Each thread handles 16 real points or 8 complex points. - Total blocks = (B*C + ...). Actually, 16384 = 2^14. With 256 threads per block, each thread handles 64 points. That's a lot for registers. With a 1024-thread block, each thread handles 16 real points in registers. We can do a Stockham FFT with shared memory communication. For 16 real points per thread, we do local butterflies first (radix-16 in registers), then use shared memory for remaining stages. There are log2(16384) = 14 stages. A radix-16 block covers stages 0-3 (since 2^4 = 16), leaving 10 stages. For the remaining stages, we use shuffle or shared memory. This is the approach used by high-performance FFT libraries like vkFFT or specialized implementations. It's complex but doable. Can we write a custom autograd function? Yes, with `torch.autograd.Function`. We'd implement `forward` using our CUDA kernel and `backward` manually. The backward pass of convolution is: - dL/dx = conv_transpose(dL/dout, filter) = circ_conv(dL/dout_padded, filter_flipped_padded) - dL/dk = conv(dL/dout_flipped, x) Actually, for this specific operation: out = ifft( fft(x_padded) * fft(k_padded) ) By chain rule: dL/dx_f = dL/dout_f * conj(k_f) (or something similar) Actually, in frequency domain, multiplication becomes convolution in time, but for the gradient: dL/dx_padded = irfft( dL/dout_f * k_f ) dL/dk_padded = irfft( sum_b( dL/dout_f[b] * x_f[b] ) ) These are the same operations! So we'd need a custom autograd Function that calls our own FFT in both forward and backward. For the forward: 1. Pad input to 16384. 2. Run custom R2C FFT. 3. Multiply by k_f. 4. Run custom C2R IFFT. 5. Slice to 8192. For backward: Given grad_output of shape [B, C, 8192]: 1. Pad grad_output to 16384. 2. Run custom R2C FFT on grad_output. 3. Compute grad_x_f = grad_out_f * k_f.conj() ??? Wait, for multiplication in frequency domain: out_f = x_f * k_f. Then dL/dx_f = dL/dout_f * k_f.conj()? No, since x_f and k_f are complex, and x_f and k_f come from real signals with specific symmetries. Let's derive it properly. If we define the operation as: y = irfft( rfft(x_padded) * rfft(k_padded) )[:L] Then in time domain, y is the circular convolution of x_padded and k_padded, truncated. The gradient w.r.t. x is the circular cross-correlation of grad_y (padded to fft_size) and k_padded. In frequency domain: grad_x_f = rfft(grad_y_padded) * conj(rfft(k_padded)) Wait, for circular convolution: y = x (*) k (where (*) is circular convolution). Then ∂y/∂x is a circulant matrix with first row k. The gradient is circulant cross-correlation: grad_x[n] = sum_m grad_y[m] * k[(m-n) mod N]. In frequency domain, cross-correlation becomes elementwise multiply with complex conjugate: FFT(grad_x) = FFT(grad_y) * conj(FFT(k)) Similarly, grad_k[n] = sum_m grad_y[m] * x[(m-n) mod N], so FFT(grad_k) = FFT(grad_y) * conj(FFT(x)) And then we need to sum over the batch dimension for grad_k. So backward is: pad grad_out to fft_size, do R2C, multiply by conj(k_f), do C2R to get grad_x_padded, then slice to L for grad_x. For grad_k: same but multiply by conj(x_f), sum over batch. This means backward requires: - 1 R2C for grad_out (B*C transforms) - Elementwise multiply for grad_x_f (B*C) - 1 C2R for grad_x (B*C transforms) - Elementwise multiply and sum over batch for grad_k_f - 1 C2R for grad_k (C transforms) That's roughly the same cost as forward. Actually, I can pass the batch size and fft_size to the C++ function, and the C++ function can cache plans in an `unordered_map` keyed by (batch, fft_size). With a static mutex. This keeps everything in C++. For thread safety in PyTorch, we might need to be careful. But since these models are typically used in a single Python thread, a simple static cache should work. Let me design the C++ API: ```cpp // Module-level plan cache struct PlanCache { std::unordered_map<int64_t, cufftHandle> r2c_plans; std::unordered_map<int64_t, cufftHandle> c2r_plans; cufftHandle get_r2c_plan(int batch, int n) { ... } cufftHandle get_c2r_plan(int batch, int n) { ... } ~PlanCache() { destroy all plans } }; static PlanCache plan_cache; torch::Tensor filter_r2c(torch::Tensor filter, int n) { int C = filter.size(0); int L = filter.size(1); int F = n / 2 + 1; auto k_padded = torch::zeros({C, n}, filter.options()); k_padded.narrow(1, 0, L).copy_(filter); auto k_f = torch::empty({C, F}, filter.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan = plan_cache.get_r2c_plan(C, n); cufftSetStream(plan, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecR2C(plan, k_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)k_f.data_ptr()); return k_f; } torch::Tensor conv_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor k_f, int n, int seq_len) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int F = n / 2 + 1; // Pad x auto x_padded = torch::zeros({B*C, n}, x.options()); x_padded.narrow(1, 0, L).copy_(x.view({B*C, L})); // FFT x auto x_f = torch::empty({B*C, F}, x.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan_fwd = plan_cache.get_r2c_plan(B*C, n); cufftSetStream(plan_fwd, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecR2C(plan_fwd, x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr()); // Multiply ...custom kernel... // IFFT auto out = torch::empty({B*C, n}, x.options()); cufftHandle plan_bwd = plan_cache.get_c2r_plan(B*C, n); cufftSetStream(plan_bwd, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecC2R(plan_bwd, (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr(), out.data_ptr<float>()); // Scale out.div_(n); return out.view({B, C, n}).narrow(2, 0, seq_len); } ``` For the multiply kernel, I should make sure grids are sized appropriately. Wait, I need to handle the fact that cuFFT C2R output overwrites the input (x_f) if we use in-place? No, `cufftExecC2R` with different input/output pointers is out-of-place. But we CAN do it in-place if the output buffer is the same size or larger. Actually, for C2R, the input is [B*C, F] complex and output is [B*C, n] real. F = n/2+1. For n=16384, F=8193. Complex input size: 8193 * 8 = 65,544 bytes per row. Real output size: 16384 * 4 = 65,536 bytes per row. Very close! In fact, cuFFT allows in-place C2R when the real array is large enough. But for simplicity, let me allocate a separate output buffer. Actually, looking at the memory: the complex array has `F = n/2+1` elements. The real array has `n` elements. In bytes: complex is `F * sizeof(complex) = (n/2+1) * 8 = 4n + 8`. Real is `n * 4 = 4n`. So complex needs 8 MORE bytes than real per row! But for in-place transforms, cuFFT requires the real array to be at least as large as the complex array in some cases. Actually, for R2C in-place, the input array must have at least `2*(n/2+1)` real elements = `n+2` real elements. So we need to pad by 2 reals. For C2R in-place from complex input, if we have an array of `F` complex elements = `2*F = n+2` real elements, that's exactly enough to hold `n` real output. So C2R can be done in-place on an array of `F` complex elements. Wait, but our x_f is allocated as [B*C, F] complex. Can we reinterpret it as [B*C, n+2] real and do C2R in-place? Actually, PyTorch uses a caching allocator, so repeated allocations of the same size reuse memory quickly. The cost is mainly the first allocation and deallocation overhead. A custom kernel with persistent buffers avoids this. Let me think about the `narrow(1, 0, L).copy_(...)` operations. These launch separate kernels. A custom kernel that does copy+pad in one go is slightly more efficient. Custom pad-copy kernel: ```cpp __global__ void pad_copy_kernel(float* dst, const float* src, int L, int n, int total) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int offset = idx / L * n + idx % L; dst[offset] = src[idx]; } ``` For zero-initialization, `torch::zeros` does a memset. Then we do the copy. That's two passes. We can do it in one: ```cpp __global__ void pad_copy_kernel(float* dst, const float* src, int L, int n, int total_src) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; // Each row: zero n elements, then copy L elements int row = idx / n; int col = idx % n; if (row >= total_src / L) return; if (col < L) { dst[row * n + col] = src[row * L + col]; } else { dst[row * n + col] = 0.0f; } } ``` This is one kernel launch that does both zeroing and copying. It saves one memory pass over the padding region. For the output slice: `out.narrow(2, 0, seq_len)` returns a view, no copy needed. OK let me start writing the code. Let me draft the full code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.utils.cpp_extension cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cufft.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h> __global__ void freq_multiply_kernel( float2* x_f, const float2* k_f, int B, int C, int F) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C * F; if (idx >= total) return; int f = idx % F; int c = (idx / F) % C; float2 xv = x_f[idx]; float2 kv = k_f[c * F + f]; float2 out; out.x = xv.x * kv.x - xv.y * kv.y; out.y = xv.x * kv.y + xv.y * kv.x; x_f[idx] = out; } __global__ void pad_copy_kernel( float* dst, const float* src, int B, int C, int L, int n) { int total_src = B * C * L; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total_src) return; int l = idx % L; int bc = idx / L; dst[bc * n + l] = src[idx]; } torch::Tensor conv_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor k_f, int fft_size, int seq_len) { TORCH_CHECK(x.is_cuda(), "x must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(k_f.is_cuda(), "k_f must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(x.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "x must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(k_f.dtype() == torch::kComplexFloat, "k_f must be complex64"); int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int F = fft_size / 2 + 1; // Create zero-padded x auto x_padded = torch::zeros({B*C, fft_size}, x.options()); auto x_view = x.reshape({B*C, L}); int threads = 256; int blocks = (B*C*L + threads - 1) / threads; pad_copy_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), x_view.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, L, fft_size); // FFT forward: x -> x_f auto x_f = torch::empty({B*C, F}, x.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan; int n = fft_size; cufftPlanMany(&plan, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, fft_size, nullptr, 1, F, CUFFT_R2C, B*C); cufftSetStream(plan, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecR2C(plan, x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr()); // Frequency domain multiply blocks = (B*C*F + threads - 1) / threads; freq_multiply_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>( (float2*)x_f.data_ptr(), (const float2*)k_f.data_ptr(), B, C, F); // FFT inverse: x_f -> out auto out = torch::empty({B*C, fft_size}, x.options()); cufftHandle plan_inv; cufftPlanMany(&plan_inv, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, F, nullptr, 1, fft_size, CUFFT_C2R, B*C); cufftSetStream(plan_inv, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecC2R(plan_inv, (cufftComplex*)x_f.data_ptr(), out.data_ptr<float>()); // Scale cuFFT output out.div_(fft_size); // Clean up cufft plans (for now; later we may cache) cufftDestroy(plan); cufftDestroy(plan_inv); return out.view({B, C, fft_size}).slice(2, 0, seq_len); } torch::Tensor filter_r2c_cuda(torch::Tensor filter, int fft_size) { TORCH_CHECK(filter.is_cuda(), "filter must be CUDA"); int C = filter.size(0); int L = filter.size(1); int F = fft_size / 2 + 1; auto k_padded = torch::zeros({C, fft_size}, filter.options()); k_padded.slice(1, 0, L).copy_(filter); auto k_f = torch::empty({C, F}, filter.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat)); cufftHandle plan; int n = fft_size; cufftPlanMany(&plan, 1, &n, nullptr, 1, fft_size, nullptr, 1, F, CUFFT_R2C, C); cufftSetStream(plan, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream()); cufftExecR2C(plan, k_padded.data_ptr<float>(), (cufftComplex*)k_f.data_ptr()); cufftDestroy(plan); return k_f; } ''' # Python wrapping... ``` Wait, `slice(2, 0, seq_len)` in C++? The reference's backward would be handled by PyTorch autograd automatically. In my custom function, I need to code it up. Let me derive the backward formulas properly. Given: y = ifft( fft(x_padded) * fft(k_padded) )[:L] Where x_padded = [x, 0], k_padded = [k, 0]. In time domain, this is circular convolution of x_padded and k_padded, truncated. cconv(x_padded, k_padded)[n] = sum_m x_padded[m] * k_padded[(n-m) mod N] The gradient w.r.t. x: ∂L/∂x_padded = cconv(grad_y_padded, reverse(k_padded)) Wait, let's be careful. For linear convolution y = x * k (where * is linear conv): ∂y[i]/∂x[j] = k[i-j] if 0 <= i-j < len(k), else 0. So ∂L/∂x[j] = sum_i grad_y[i] * k[i-j] for valid i. This is the cross-correlation of grad_y and k. ∂L/∂x[j] = sum_m grad_y[j+m] * k[m] = (grad_y ⋆ k)[j] Where ⋆ is cross-correlation (not convolution). Cross-correlation can be written as convolution with flipped k: (grad_y ⋆ k)[j] = (grad_y * flip(k))[j] In frequency domain, cross-correlation corresponds to multiplication with conj(K): FFT(cross-correlation) = conj(K_f) * G_f where G_f = FFT(grad_y_padded) and K_f = FFT(k_padded). Actually, for circular cross-correlation: c = ifft( fft(a) * conj(fft(b)) ) So grad_x_padded = ifft( fft(grad_y_padded) * conj(fft(k_padded)) ) And for grad_k: grad_k_padded = ifft( fft(grad_y_padded) * conj(fft(x_padded)) ) summed over the batch dimension. So backward requires: 1. Pad grad_output to fft_size 2. R2C of grad_output_padded 3. Conj(k_f) * grad_out_f -> grad_x_f 4. C2R of grad_x_f 5. Slice to get grad_x Similarly for grad_k: 1. Conj(x_f) * grad_out_f (for each batch) 2. Sum over batch 3. C2R 4. Slice These operations are very similar to forward, just with conjugation and batch sum. We could write custom CUDA kernels for backward too, but PyTorch already handles this efficiently. If we use PyTorch ops for backward, the backward pass might be similar speed to the reference model's backward, but that's fine since we're optimizing forward. Actually, for the backward pass, we only need to compute gradients. Using PyTorch ops for backward while using custom CUDA for forward is a great trade-off. So I'll write: ```python class FFTConvFunc(torch.autograd.Function): @staticmethod def forward(ctx, x, filter, fft_size, seq_len): k_f = _ext.filter_r2c_cuda(filter, fft_size) out = _ext.conv_forward_cuda(x, k_f, fft_size, seq_len) ctx.fft_size = fft_size ctx.seq_len = seq_len ctx.save_for_backward(x, filter) return out @staticmethod def backward(ctx, grad_output): x, filter = ctx.saved_tensors N = ctx.fft_size L = ctx.seq_len # This is equivalent to PyTorch's autograd through the reference # We can just call the reference logic for backward grad_padded = torch.nn.functional.pad(grad_output, (0, L)) grad_f = torch.fft.rfft(grad_padded, dim=-1) k_padded = torch.nn.functional.pad(filter, (0, L)) k_f = torch.fft.rfft(k_padded, dim=-1) x_padded = torch.nn.functional.pad(x, (0, L)) x_f = torch.fft.rfft(x_padded, dim=-1) # grad w.r.t. x grad_x_f = grad_f * torch.conj(k_f).unsqueeze(0) grad_x = torch.fft.irfft(grad_x_f, n=N, dim=-1)[..., :L] # grad w.r.t. filter grad_k_f = (grad_f * torch.conj(x_f)).sum(dim=0) grad_k = torch.fft.irfft(grad_k_f, n=N, dim=-1)[..., :L] return grad_x, grad_k, None, None ``` This gives us custom fast forward + standard backward. CUFFT_R2C, batch);\n r2c[k] = plan;\n return plan;\n }\n \n cufftHandle get_c2r(int batch, int n) {\n int64_t k = key(batch, n);\n auto it = c2r.find(k);\n if (it != c2r.end()) return it->second;\n g_plan_cache;\n\n__global__ void pad_copy_kernel(\n float* dst, const float* src,\n int B, int C, int L, int n) {\n int total = B * C * n;\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n \n int l = idx % n;\n int bc = idx / n;\n if (l < L) {\n dst[idx] = src[bc * L + l];\n } else {\n dst[idx] = 0.0f;\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void freq_multiply_kernel(\n float2* x_f,\n const float2* k_f,\n int B, int C, int F) {\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int total = B * C * F;\n if (idx >= total) return;\n \n int c = (idx / F) % C;\n \n float2 xv = x_f[idx];\n float2 kv = k_f[c * F + idx % F];\n // NOTE: kv should be indexed by (c * F + f), but f = idx % F\n // Recompute f correctly:\n int f = idx % F;\n kv = k_f[c * F + f];\n \n float2 out;\n int64_t F = fft_size / 2 + 1;\n \n // 1. Compute filter FFT\n auto k_f = filter_r2c_cuda(filter, fft_size);\n \n // 2. Pad and copy x\n auto x_padded = torch::empty({B * C, fft_size}, x.options());\n int total_pad = B * C * fft_size;\n int threads = 256;\n int blocks = (total_pad + threads - 1) / threads;\n pad_copy_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()>>>(\n x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), x.reshape({B * C, L}).data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, L, fft_size);\n \n // 3. FFT x\n auto x_f = torch::empty({B * C, F}, x.options().dtype(torch::kComplexFloat));\n cufftHandle plan_fwd = g_plan_cache.get_r2c(B * C, fft_size);\n cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream().stream();\n cufftSetStream(plan_fwd, stream);\n cufftExecR2C(plan_fwd, x_padded.data_ptr<float>(), reinterpret_cast<cufftComplex*>(x_f.data_ptr()));\n \n // 4. Frequency multiply\n int total_f = B * C * F;\n blocks = (total_f + threads - 1) / threads;\n freq_multiply_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, stream>>>(\n reinterpret_cast<float2*>(x_f.data_ptr()),\n reinterpret_cast<const float2*>(k_f.data_ptr()),\n B, C, F);\n \n // 5. Inverse FFT\n auto out = torch::empty({B * C, fft_size}, x.options());\n cufftHandle plan_bwd = g_plan_cache.get_c2r(B * C, fft_size);\n cufftSetStream(plan_bwd, stream);\n cufftExecC2R(plan_bwd, reinterpret_cast<cufftComplex*>(x_f.data_ptr()), out.data_ptr<float>());\n \n // 6. Scale by 1/fft_size\n int total_out = B * C * fft_size;\n blocks = (total_out + threads - 1) / threads;\n float scale = 1.0f / static_cast<float>(fft_size);\n scale_kernel<<<blocks, threads, 0, stream>>>(out.data_ptr<float>(), total_out, scale);\n \n return out.view({B, C, fft_size}).narrow(2, 0, seq_len);\n}\n'''\n\n# Compile the extension\n_ext = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(\n name='custom_fft_conv',\n cpp_sources='',\n cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,\n functions=['filter_r2c_cuda', 'conv_forward_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n extra_ldflags=['-lcufft'],\n verbose=False,\n is_python_module=True,\n)\n\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n \"\"\"\n Optimized 1D convolution via FFT using custom CUDA kernels and cuFFT.\n \"\"\"\n def __init__(self, channels: int, seq_len: int):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.channels = channels\n self.seq_len = seq_len\n self.fft_size = 2 * seq_len\n self.filter = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(channels, seq_len))\n # When `cpp_sources=''`, it generates the pybind code in a standalone main.cpp but doesn't include the cuda.h or declarations. The .cu file is compiled separately and linked later. It does compile `cpp_sources` as C++ and `cuda_sources` as CUDA, then links them together. But main.cpp needs to see the declarations to compile. Let me create proper `cpp_sources` with forward declarations.

reasoning
The CXXABI error is likely due to the environment using a newer C++ standard library than what's available in /lib/x86_64-linux-gnu. The load_inline default adds torch include paths and compiles with -std=c++17, which pulls in newer symbols. The previous attempt to pass -D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0 failed because the ABI flag matched PyTorch but the resulting .so needed newer symbols. A common workaround is to use extra_ldflags to force linking against the newer libstdc++ inside the environment, or to omit such flags. However, looking at the error location, it's the Python import loading the `.so` where libstdc++ is used. The system might have a newer one under /opt/conda/lib or similar. But we can't control LD_LIBRARY_PATH. Alternative: Don't compile a custom C++ extension at all. Instead, write a pure Triton kernel that does the frequency multiplication and use PyTorch's built-in FFT functions for the actual transforms. That avoids C++ extensions entirely and only needs `triton` (should be installed). But wait — PyTorch's built-in `torch.fft.rfft` is likely highly optimized already. The only thing we might beat is overhead. Or we could use `torch.fft.rfftn` with batched dims, or use `torch.fft.rfft` with batch dimension already collapsed. The reference uses: - pad x to (B, C, L*2) - pad filter to (C, L*2) - x_f = torch.fft.rfft(x_padded, dim=-1) -> (B, C, F) - k_f = torch.fft.rfft(k_padded, dim=-1) -> (C, F) - multiply with broadcasting - irfft Let's think: What is slow about the reference? It pads x and filter every time. The filter is a parameter, so we could precompute its FFT in `__init__` if the filter doesn't change. But during training it changes every step. However, if we precompute the filter FFT each forward, we still avoid the `pad` for the filter if we maintain it in padded form. But the problem says `ModelNew` must accept same constructor args, implement `forward()` with same signature, and produce numerically equivalent outputs. We can add attributes and set up differently, but we must produce the same output. Actually, PyTorch's FFT is very heavily optimized using cuFFT. The bottleneck might be memory bandwidth for padding, data movement, and the Python-level overhead. But maybe the PyTorch reference is fine and we just need to avoid C++ extension issues. Wait — what if the expected speedup doesn't come from custom FFT plans but from a different algorithm? Let's analyze the operation: `output = conv1d(x, filter)` where each channel has its own filter, but filter is not batched across B. The reference computes: - pad x: [B, C, 2L] - FFT: [B, C, 2L] -> [B, C, F] - pad filter: [C, 2L] - FFT: [C, 2L] -> [C, F] - multiply: broadcast over B - IFFT: [B, C, F] -> [B, C, 2L] - slice: [B, C, L] Since FFT size is 2L = 16384, which is power of 2, cuFFT is very efficient. One optimization: don't re-FFT the filter every time if `self.filter` hasn't changed. During inference, it won't change. During training, it changes each iteration due to gradient updates, but we could still check if the filter tensor data hash/version changed. However, PyTorch's `nn.Parameter` has `_version` which increments on in-place operations. Gradient updates via `.step()` might not be in-place if the optimizer creates a new `.data`? Actually `param.data = ...` is `_version` safe? `tensor.add_()` increments `_version`. SGD with momentum might do in-place `p.data.add_(...)`. So we can cache the FFT of the filter using _version. But let's first try avoiding the C++ extension and using PyTorch ops more efficiently. Idea: 1. Don't pad x using `F.pad` but using `torch.cat([x, torch.zeros(...)], dim=-1)`. This avoids the overhead of nn.functional.pad wrapper but same memory copy. 2. Reshape x to [B*C, 2L] before FFT to have larger batch transform. cuFFT may handle larger batch better? Actually pytorch `torch.fft.rfft` already operates on contiguous last dim. 3. Similarly reshape filter to [C, 2L] and FFT. 4. Multiply, IFFT, reshape. The reference already does explicit unsqueeze(0) for k_f, and `*`, so broadcasting might have some overhead. We can fuse the multiply manually. But all these are minor. On H100, the reference likely runs fast (~100 us?). To beat it, we might need a custom kernel only for the fusion parts and rely on PyTorch cuFFT for the heavy math. Actually, the `torch::Tensor` object is passed by value into the C++ function and lives for the duration of the call, so `.data_ptr()` is valid during the kernel launch. The reference implementation: 1. `F.pad(x, (0, s))` - allocates `{B, C, 2L}` 2. `F.pad(filter, (0, s))` - allocates `{C, 2L}` 3. `torch.fft.rfft(x_padded, dim=-1)` - allocates `{B, C, L+1}` complex 4. `torch.fft.rfft(k_padded, dim=-1)` - allocates `{C, L+1}` complex 5. `x_f * k_f.unsqueeze(0)` - allocates output 6. `torch.fft.irfft(...)` - allocates output `{B, C, 2L}` 7. `out[..., :seq_len]` - allocates a view (slice), but returns a contiguous tensor? Actually slicing creates a view. But the IRFFT result might be contiguous. So there are lots of intermediate allocations. Our kernel: 1. `torch::zeros({C, fft_size})` 2. `torch::empty({C, F})` 3. cufft r2c for filter 4. `torch::empty({BC, fft_size})` 5. `torch::empty({BC, F})` 6. cufft r2c for x 7. freq multiply in-place 8. `torch::empty({BC, fft_size})` 9. cufft c2r 10. `torch::empty({BC, seq_len})` So we also have allocations, but maybe fewer temporaries and less Python overhead. Also PyTorch's `rfft` might use more general plans, while our static cache persists.
